The value of $ = 1$ if the expression $ $ is true, otherwise it is 0. The range of HD is between 0 and 1 . } MME is based on the envy-freeness concept, which deems a recommendation fair if no item is disadvantaged by its own exposure allocation, compared to having any other item's exposure allocation. At a high level, MME can be said to quantify unfairness as the disadvantage suffered by an item if its exposure allocation is swapped with that of another item. The disadvantage is measured based on an impact score, which depends on item exposure and relevance: given the full recommendation lists, $L_u^n$ across all users, each item $i$ is first swapped with another item $i'$. For each item, the pre- and post-swap impact scores are computed. If item $i$'s pre-swap score is not less than its post-swap score, we have envy-freeness for item $i$ w.r.t.~item $i'$. MME averages the maximum difference in the impact imposed if item $i$'s exposure allocation is swapped with that of another item $i'$. MME is defined as follows: _ = {n} _{i I} \{ _{i' I} Imp_i - Imp_i \} Imp_i = _{u U} _{p=1}^k r_{u,i} e_{} X_{u,i',p} X_{u,i',p} = {W} {m} _{w=1}^W 1_{L_{u,w}} =p) where $Imp_i $ is the impact of item $i$ when its exposure allocation is swapped with that of item $i'$, $X_{u,i',p}$ is the probability of recommending item $i'$ to user $u$ at position $p$ across $W$ recommendation rounds, and $e_{} $ is the exposure weight of item at position $p$, where the weight is discounted with the inverse examination function . The range of MME is $[0, )$. } IBO and IWO are based on the principle of , where fairness is achieved when every item has a higher impact under the current ranking policy, than if it were under the uniform random ranking policy $ _{unif}$, which randomly samples all possible item permutations. IBO/IWO is the percentage of items for which the current ranking policy improves/worsens impact by at least 10\ _ = {n} _{i I} ( }{Imp^{unif}_i} 1.1 ) _ = {n} _{i I} ( }{Imp^{unif}_i} 0.9 ) Imp^{unif}_{i} = {m}{n} _{p=1}^{k} {p} _{u U} r_{u,i} $Imp_i $ is as per Eq.~ and $Imp^{unif}_i$ is the impact item $i$ has under the ranking policy $ _{unif}$, with $e_{} $ as the examination function . The range of IBO/IWO is $ $. } II-F was first proposed by ~ as an unfairness measure in ranking, where unfairness is the difference between system exposure and target exposure of individual queries and individual items. II-F was reformulated for RSs as: _ = {m} {n} _{u U} _{i I} ^2 E_{u,i} = {W} _{w=1}^{W}1_{L_{u,w}} e_{} E_{u,i}^{*} = }{|R_u^*|} }{1- } \, |R_u^*|>0\, 0 where $E_{u,i}$ is the system exposure, i.e., the expected exposure of $i$ to $u$ under a stochastic ranking policy. $E_{u,i}^{*}$ is the target exposure, i.e., the expected exposure of $i$ to $u$ under an ideal stochastic ranking policy, where relevant items receive equal expected exposure~. As such, II-F deems a recommendation fair when the system-allocated item exposure is equal to the exposure allocated to items by the ideal stochastic ranking policy. The RBP-based examination function is used in $E_{u,i}$ and the formulation of $E_{u,i}^{*}$ also depends on this examination function . $|R_u^*|$ is the number of relevant items for user $u$. II-F ranges between $ $. } AI-F quantifies how much RSs~ under/overexpose an item to all users by averaging the deviation of the overall system exposure from a target exposure: _ = {n} _{i I} ( {m} _{u U} E_{u,i}^{ } - {m} _{u U} E_{u,i}^{*} )^2 where the system exposure $E_{u,i}^{ }$ and the target exposure $E_{u,i}^{*}$ are given in {ext_eq:eui-star} . As with II-F, AI-F also evaluates fairness based on the disparity between the system exposure and the target exposure. While this difference is computed per user-item pair in II-F, AI-F computes the exposure difference after summing item exposure across users. Consequently, given a larger percentage of unique items in the recommendation , AI-F would give a fairer score than II-F. AI-F has a range of $ $. The measures in suffer from theoretical limitations. Following ~, the term ``limitation'' refers to a measure failing to quantify or fulfill criteria that are important in fairness evaluation. Regardless of the reason , the limitation restricts the usage of the measure. Several limitations may render the measure completely unusable , but others can still be used with an awareness of the limitation and how it affects the measure or its interpretation . Here, we consider measure usage in the case of top-$k$ recommendations, as one of the most popular recommendation scenarios. Altogether, we identify five limitations affecting the measures, summarised in . In each heading below, we put in brackets the names of the measures affected by the limitation. in this table. Source refers to where the limitation is first identified. Empty cells mean that the measure does not suffer from the limitation. } {!}{ {l|c||c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c} {0.6 } {Legend\\ : we fully resolve the limitation\\ : we partially resolve the limitation for single-round and binary relevance cases \\ : the limitation is unresolvable } & {90}{Source} & {90}{IAA } & {90}{IFD$_{ }$ } & {90}{IFD$_{ }$ } & {90}{HD } & {90}{MME } & {90}{IBO/IWO } & {90}{II-F } & {90}{AI-F } \\ Non-realisability: cannot reach max/min score &&&&&&&&&\\ Non-realisability due to unknown formulation of max/min score &&&&& & & && \\ Non-uniform exposure weight&us& & & &&&& & \\ Non-localisation: requires item relevance information beyond cut-off $k$ && & && && & & \\ Undefinedness: cannot be computed && &&&&& &&\\ Zero-exposure: gives zero exposure weight to exposed item &us& &&&&&&&\\ Top-$k$-insensitivity: gives nonzero exposure weight to unexposed item $k$ &us&& &&&&&&\\ } All measures are affected by this limitation, which is defined as the measure theoretically failing to reach the max/min score, given all possible item rankings across users . The non-realisability limitation causes difficulty in interpreting the fairness score, as the best or worst possible fairness score changes depending on the dataset distribution, , or experimental choices . Thus, how far the fairness score is from the max or min score cannot be known. For example, if a lower-is-fairer measure has a -range and a model scores 0.2, one might think that the model is not perfectly fair and that fairness can still be improved. Yet, if a score of 0.2 is already the minimum that can be achieved given any combination of values, it would mean that the recommendation cannot be made fairer; none of the measures scores can show this and their scores may therefore be misleading. In other words, this means that the achievable measure range may not actually be as per the theoretical range, but only a subrange of it. This limitation is first defined in ~ in relation to the number of recommendation slots and the number of items in the dataset. Here, we show that the same limitation occurs in relation to the item relevance labels and exposure weights. Four causes of non-realisability were previously identified in ~. We also identify a new cause of non-realisability in the measures. Next, we explain the two causes of non-realisability, one that has been identified in ~ and the new cause that we identified. The first cause of non-realisability is due to the exact formulation of the max/min achievable score being unknown : given a set of user-item relevance labels, how do we order items in the recommendation list to produce the maximum or minimum fairness score? In other words, the fairest recommendation cannot be determined solely through theoretical analysis due to the complexity of the measure . While one can always enumerate all permutations to get the achievable max/min scores, it is a highly costly process; thus, in the examples below, we use toy examples with a small number of users and items. Even if our hypothetical examples here are only with small values, we argue that a fairness measure should still work properly given any number of users or items. Further, in real-life settings, there are recommendation use cases with only a few items, e.g., insurance product recommendation . Evaluating fairness in such cases would need a fairness measure that works properly for a small number of items. Note that all measures affected by this limitation except HD are defined for multiple rounds of recommendations, but since MME, IBO, and IWO have only been computed under cases equivalent to a single round of recommendation , we only provide examples for a single recommendation round for these measures. In principle, HD ranges between 0 and 1, but for $k=m=2$ and $n=3$, its maximum score is only 0.707, which is achieved when all top-$k$ items are irrelevant. This maximum score is obtained after iterating through all permutations of the recommendation list and item relevance values while assuming binary relevance $r_{u,i} \{0,1\}$. Given the score of 0.707, when comparing it to the theoretical range of , one may think that the recommendation is rather unfair, but not severely unfair, even if this is the most unfair score that is empirically achievable given the experimental choice and dataset composition. Hence, the non-realisability may hinder an accurate representation of the fairness level. The most fair MME, $=0$, means that recommendations are envy-free, which we explain in . For $k>1$, an envy-free recommendation is not always guaranteed , so $=0$ may be unreachable. For example, when $k=n=3$, $m=2$, and when each item is relevant to all users, the minimum achievable MME is ${18}$ under a deterministic ranking policy. While the upper bound of MME is unclear, the fairest scores for IBO and IWO , are not always achievable. For example, under a deterministic ranking policy, when $k=n=3$, $m=2$, and for any combinations of relevance values for all users and items, the maximum achievable MME for any permutations of recommendation lists across all users is ${18}$, the fairest IBO is ${3}$, and the fairest IWO is ${3}$. Both IBO/ IWO compare the impact, based on exposure and relevance, produced by a recommendation list to the thresholded impact, based on the impact produced by recommendation lists with uniform exposure. The thresholded impact depends on a multiplying constant which is set arbitrarily and a priori . It is thus possible that the optimal distribution of impact in our recommendation list is sometimes less than the thresholded impact or more than the thresholded impact due to the dataset composition. Intuitively, not all items may have a better impact than the thresholded impact, which causes IBO $<1$, and not all items would have a worse impact than the thresholded impact, which causes IWO $>0$. So, comparing impacts as above may cause the fairest score to be unreachable. AI-F may also not reach the unfairest score of 1. For example, given $k=m=2, n=3$, for all permutations of items in a possible recommendation list and user-item binary relevance values, its maximum score is only 0.88. This score is obtained when the same $k$ items are recommended to all users, and both items are irrelevant to each user. Even if the setup is extended to multi-round settings, the maximum AI-F would remain 0.88, obtained from recommending the same $k$ items to all users in all rounds. This is because the target exposure $E_{u,i}^*$ is independent of the number of rounds, while the system exposure $E_{u,i}$ producing the unfairest AI-F in multi-round settings is the same as that of the single-round, as it produces the maximum aggregated difference with the target exposure. $, IFD$_{ }$, II-F)} We identify a second cause of non-realisability, which is due to the items having non-uniform exposure. All measures affected by this limitation compare and combine item exposure and relevance through some functions . When the exposure weight and relevance do not change at the same rate down the ranks, aggregating these scores at the user/item level, e.g., through computing their differences, will not be exactly zero. As such, the resulting fairness score will also have some residuals due to this discrepancy. As a result, a lower-is-fairer score that should have been 0 may not be achieved, leading to the misinterpretation that the recommendation is not completely fair, even if it is already the fairest possible, given the dataset composition. For IAA, in an ideal effectiveness-based ranking, relevance and exposure weight may decrease at different rates down the rank. This causes the fairest score to be unreachable, as the minimum score cannot be achieved if the normalised relevance value does not decrease at the same rate as the examination function down the ranks. For example, for $k=2, n=4$, assume a user's full recommendation list with normalised relevance values of $ $, and the list of normalised exposure weights given by the linear examination function $ $. The two exposed items are with the highest relevance among the 4 items, but $_{} ={4} = 0.2 > 0$ even though the recommendations are made as fair as possible. The issue persists when extending this to a multi-round scenario . In the previous example, the fairest $ $ remains 0.2 for any $W$, as the recommendation list is already ordered by decreasing relevance, assuming the relevance $r_{u,i,w}$ for a specific user-item pair stays constant throughout different rounds. Similarly, the most unfair score cannot be achieved if the normalised relevance value does not decrease at the same rate as the exposure weight. In this case, the maximum IAA is only 0.7 instead of 1. IFD$_{ }$ and IFD$_{ }$ may neither reach the fairest score of 0 nor the unfairest score of 1. Under a binary relevance scenario, for $k=n=10$, considering all possible combinations of recommendation list and item relevance values, the fairest IFD$_{ } $ is 0.003, while the unfairest is 0.158. Similarly, the fairest IFD$_{ } $ can only be 0.0167 and the unfairest is at most 0.265. For non-binary relevance, suppose $r_{u,i} \{0,0.5,1\}$, IFD$_{ } $ can only be 0 when there are exactly two items with nonzero relevance. Otherwise, its minimum is 0.006, and its maximum is 0.428. Under the same setting, IFD$_{ } $ is bounded between 0.004 and 0.265. For similar reasons, these issues would also extend to multi-round scenarios, even though the lower/upper bounds may change fairer score than with single rounds). The unfairest and fairest II-F score may not be achievable either. For instance, given $k=m=2, n=3$, for all possible recommendation lists and item relevance values , the maximum II-F is only 0.88 , while its minimum is 0.007 . We also enumerate all possibilities for two and three recommendation rounds. The minimum II-F can be 0 across two rounds, but it is still not exactly 0, if there are three rounds. However, the maximum II-F will still be 0.88 in either case, for the same reason as we explain for AI-F in . Hence, even in multi-round scenarios, the theoretical max/min II-F score may not be achievable. $}{}, HD, IBO/ IWO, II-F, AI-F)} The non-localisation limitation is based on the localisation property of effectiveness measures in Information Retrieval . The localisation property is defined as ``A score at depth $k$ can be computed based solely on knowledge of the documents that appear in the top-$k$'' . We redefine the property as a measure limitation in RSs, where the measure requires item relevance information beyond the top-$k$. While this may be a deliberate design choice, it is still a limitation, as having additional relevant items beyond the top-$k$ may cause the measure score to appear fairer than if there are no additional relevant items. In practice, this may lead to overestimation of fairness, and instability in the evaluation results. Since most RS datasets are highly sparse, with only a few relevant items per user, their fairness evaluation can also be affected by this limitation. We explain the limitation through an example. Suppose that given $k=2, n=5$, a user's full recommendation list $L_u^n$ has the normalised relevance values of $ $, where a `$?$' represents unknown relevance, and the list of normalised exposure weights given by the linear examination function is $ $. The relevance of an item may be unknown due to, for example, the item not being shown to the user or the item being shown to the user but not rated/interacted with. We refer to items with unknown relevance as unobserved items henceforth. Following the convention in IR evaluation , unobserved items are usually regarded conservatively as irrelevant. In this case, IAA$ =0.2$. However, if all unobserved items are actually relevant instead, IAA$ $ would be 0.8, and thus the difference from the actual score is 0.6.~.} Considering that IAA ranges in $ $, the large difference is worrying, as a score of 0.8 could be interpreted as highly unfair, while 0.2 is rather fair. Even if there is only one unobserved item, the difference in IAA based on the two ways of treating the unobserved items would be 0.2, a non-negligible difference. Using the same example as the one used for IAA, we show that IFD$_ $ also suffers from the same limitation. When all unobserved items are regarded as irrelevant, IFD$_ =0.092$. The opposite treatment will result in a higher IFD$_ $, i.e., 0.114. Worse, having only one additional relevant item can cause an even higher difference in the IFD$_ $ scores. If the additional relevant item is at position $n$, e.g., $ $, IFD$_ =0.136$. IBO, IWO, II-F, AI-F, and HD also suffer from non-localisation. Item relevance information of all user-item pairs is required to compute for IBO/IWO and to compute for II-F/AI-F. On the other hand, HD technically requires only the top-$k$ items with the highest ground truth relevance per user . However, obtaining this list of items requires sorting the ground truth relevance of all items, regardless of their position in the predicted ranking. The undefinedness limitation is defined as the measure being incomputable, e.g., the measure giving an undefined value when encountering a specific case . In both IAA and IBO/IWO, this happens due to division by zero. Consequently, fairness cannot be evaluated as the measure does not yield any scores. For IAA, when $k=1$, the denominator of the linear examination function $_{} $ is $0$. The choice of $k=1$ can be seen on mobile phone applications, where there is limited screen space and thus only the top recommendation is presented to users or in conversational RSs . For IBO/IWO, a division by zero occurs when $Imp^{unif}_i=0$. This happens when item $i$ is not relevant for any users . Note that IFD$_{ }$ has been stated to have the zero denominator problem when encountering irrelevant items with $r_{u,i}=0$ , but this is actually false. The original measure only computes fairness based on relevant items. Hence, IFD$_{ }$ does not suffer from the undefinedness limitation. We exclude the trivial, unrealistic cases where there are no users or no items for this limitation, as evaluating fairness under these scenarios does not make sense. In addition, we identify the following other cases that cause a measure to be undefined. IFD$_{ }$ is undefined when $n<2$, as it is a pairwise measure. IFD$_{ }$ is undefined if there is no relevant item for a user as the divisor in will be zero..} Similarly, HD requires at least one relevant item per user, otherwise, the divisor in will be zero. However, we do not consider these measures to suffer from the undefinedness limitation for the following reasons. IFD$_ $ does not suffer from undefinedness for $n<2$ as evaluating item fairness when there is only one item does not make sense as only one item can be recommended to any users. IFD$_{ }$ and HD also do not suffer from undefinedness for users with zero relevant items, as such users are generally not included in the evaluation, which is also the case when evaluating effectiveness for RSs. We define the zero-exposure limitation as the measure's examination function giving zero exposure weight to an item that is recommended at the top-$k$. While this may arguably be an intentional design choice, it is unintuitive and confusing as there is no difference between the exposure weight assigned to an exposed item and an unexposed item. For IAA, in , $_{} = 0$ for an item at position $k$. The zero value is due to min-max normalisation that re-scales all exposure weights of the top-$k$ recommended items within $ $. However, an item at $k$ is still exposed; weighing the item with a nonzero exposure value makes more sense, to distinguish its weight from the unexposed item at position $k+1$. In practice, assigning zero exposure weight for an item at position $k$ may result in an underestimation of fairness if the item is relevant and vice versa. For example, if an item at $k=10$ has a relevance value of 1, having a positive exposure weight at that position would result in a 0.9 difference between the item exposure weight and the item relevance. If the exposure weight is 0 instead, the difference would be larger . In this case, the IAA score is higher than it should be. {}-insensitivity }{}} We define the limitation of top-$k$-insensitivity as the measure's inability to respond to the change in cut-off $k$. This means that given a set of recommendations across users, the fairness score would remain the same given any values of $k$. A measure that cannot reflect a change in fairness level given a different $k$ is not ideal, as recommendations are commonly evaluated for both effectiveness and fairness at different cut-offs. This limitation can also be seen as the inverse of the zero-exposure limitation, in the sense that a nonzero exposure weight is assigned to an unexposed item. IFD$_{ }$ suffers from this limitation as does not incorporate the function $1_{L_{u,w}} $, which indicates whether item $i$ is recommended at top-$k$. Consequently, items at position $k+1$ and beyond are assigned nonzero exposure weights, which is unintuitive. For example, given $n=3$, suppose a user $u$'s full recommendation list, $L_u^n$ has the following relevance values $ $. The exposure weights for the three items based on $e_{}$ are $ $. In this case, given any $k \{1,2,3\}$, IFD$_{ }=0.033$. Yet, we may obtain a different IFD$_{ }$ score if we assign zero exposure weights to the unexposed items. Suppose $k=2$, the exposure weights should have been $ $ instead and IFD$_{ }=0.158$, which is higher than the first score. With $k=1$, IFD$_{ }=0$, which is lower than the initial score. Hence, this limitation may lead to the under/overestimation of fairness, as the IFD$_{ }$ score is lower/higher than it should be. We show how to resolve some of the measure limitations, either fully or partially, and explain why some remain unresolvable . To distinguish between the original version of an evaluation measure $M$ and our modification of it, we use $M_$ and $M_$, respectively. When referring to the measure in general, we omit the $ _$ or $ _$ subscripts. {}-insensitivity } We resolve the limitation for the only measure that suffers from it, IFD$_ $, and we partially resolve due to Cause 2 for all measures that suffer from the limitation under Cause 2 . We resolve top-$k$-insensitivity by employing the top-$k$ indicator function in the measure. We partially resolve non-realisability by normalising the measure scores based on their theoretically achievable minimum and maximum values. Specifically, we min-max normalise the score per user, using the ideal and worst recommendation list , similar to the idea of normalising DCG . As a result of the normalisation, the scores 0 and 1 refer to the fairest and unfairest possible recommendations, respectively, and the fairest recommendation is unique for each measure. We provide the intuition on obtaining the fairest recommendation list in this subsection, and the mathematical workings in the appendix. In some cases, it is difficult to identify the fairest recommendation directly from the measure equations, so we simulate all possible rankings for various data distributions and experimental choices . We then compute the measure and identify the rankings that produce the fairest scores. summarises the strategies to achieve the fairest recommendations per measure. We resolve the non-realisability limitation only for single-round cases and for binary relevance scenarios, as the aggregation across rounds and non-binary relevance introduce extra complexity in formulating the fairest recommendation list. While this may seem like a constrained setting, both scenarios are popular in RS evaluation . Furthermore, we also exclude the highly unlikely scenario where the relevance value of all $n$ items are the same for a user $u$ . measures.} {!}{ {l|*{2}{p{6cm}}} Measure & Fairest & Unfairest \\ IAA $ $ & As many relevant items as possible at the top-$k$ & As few relevant items as possible at the top-$k$ \\ II-F $ $ & As many relevant items as possible at the top-$k$ & As few relevant items as possible at the top-$k$ \\ IFD$_{ } $ & As few relevant items as possible at the top-$k$ & Half of the relevant items at the top; the other half unexposed \\ IFD$_{ } $ & As few relevant items as possible at the top-$k$ & $^*$As many relevant items as possible at the top-$k$ \\ } {0.95 } { *Works for most cases, but not all. See .} To resolve the non-realisability limitation in IAA, we compute IAA$_{ } $, i.e., the fairest possible IAA for user $u$, and IAA$_{ } $, the unfairest possible IAA for $u$. The fairest IAA$ $ is obtained from a recommendation list that is based on monotonically decreasing item relevance for the user, while the unfairest IAA$ $ is based on a list with monotonically increasing item relevance. To minimise IAA$ $, we have to minimise the absolute difference between each item's relevance score and its exposure weight. Intuitively, all relevant items should be placed at the top of the list, as item exposure weights at the top-$k$ are nonzero, and the irrelevant items should be recommended beyond the top-$k$, where the exposure weights are zero. In contrast, maximising IAA$ $ requires doing the opposite, i.e., placing the irrelevant items at the top and the relevant items at the bottom. We reformulate IAA as follows: _{} = {m} _{u U}_{} _{} = _ -_{ } }{_{ } - _{ } } where all IAA$_{ } $ in are computed with the corrected examination function, which we explain later in . {})} IFD$_{ }$ suffers from as it does not employ the function that indicates whether an item $i$ is in the top-$k$ recommendations for user $u$ in round $w$, $1_{L_{u,w}} $. As such, we resolve this limitation by incorporating the indicator function in the $J_{ } $ function, which combines item exposure and relevance. We reformulate $J_{ } $ as follows: J_{ } = {W} _{w=1}^{W} } e_{} }{r_{u,i,w}} The above modification zeros out the combined item exposure and item relevance values for items that are ranked beyond the top-$k$. Hence, given a different cut-off $k$, IFD$_{ }$ can more accurately quantify the disparity between the combined exposure-relevance scores of items in and out of the top-$k$. As IFD$_{ }$ also suffers from non-realisability, we explain how to normalise its score per user. To obtain the fairest IFD$_{ } $, i.e., IFD$_{ } $, we place all relevant items at the bottom of the list, as IFD$_{ }$ takes the pairwise difference of the exposure weight of only the relevant items . Thus, when the relevant items are at the bottom of the list, their exposure weights are 0, resulting in the least IFD$_{ } $ score. Obtaining the recommendation list with the unfairest IFD$_{ } $, i.e., IFD$_{ } $, is not as straightforward. For the base case where there are two relevant items for a user , the maximum difference can be obtained when placing one relevant item at the top, and the other one at the bottom. $ score, this is just one possibility.} Generalising to an even number of $|R_u^*|$, following the same idea, placing half of the relevant items at the top and the rest at the bottom should achieve the maximum IFD$_{ } $. For example, if the dataset has $n=8$ items, and 4 are relevant to $u$, the items should be placed such that the item relevance is ordered in the following way: $ $. If $|R_u^*|$ is odd, $|R_u^*|$-1 of the items can be recommended with the same strategy for an even number of $|R_u^*|$. The positioning of the remaining item can be closer to either the top or the bottom, e.g., $ $ or $ $. Through a simulation with various numbers of items, $n \{10, 15, 20\}$, cut-off $k \{1, 3, 5, 10\}$, and an odd number of relevant items, $|R_u^*| \{3,5, ,k-1\}$, we find that placing the item either way always produces the unfairest IFD$_{ } $. We reformulate IFD$_{ } $ as follows: _{ } = {m} _{u U}_{ } _{ } = _{ } -_{ } }{_{ } - _{ } } where all IFD$_{ } $ in are computed with our reformulation of the function combining item exposure and relevance, $J_{ }$ . Note that this normalisation only applies to users with at least two relevant items . As IFD$_ $ takes the pairwise difference between the combined exposure-relevance value of a user's relevant items, IFD$_{ } $ is always zero when a user only has one relevant item. One way to treat such users is to assign IFD$_{ } =0$ . Alternatively, they can also be excluded from the evaluation. As their scores would always be zero, these users would pull the score of IFD$_{ }$ down, making the score seem fairer than it should be. {})} For IFD$_{ }$, we obtain the fairest IFD$_{ } $ through the same recommendation list that produces IFD$_{ } $, i.e., all relevant items are at the bottom. As items at the bottom have less exposure weight than the ones at the top, this ranking minimises the pairwise difference of the item scores, which are the item exposure weight times its relevance. Formulating the item ranking with the unfairest IFD$_{ }$, i.e., IFD$_{ } $ is not feasible through analysing the measure equation. To find the ranking that produces IFD$_{ } $, we simulate rankings with various numbers of items, $n \{10, 15, 20\}$, cut-off $k \{1, 3, 5, 10\}$, and relevant items, $|R_u^*| \{2,3, ,k-1\}$. For each combination of the experimental choices, we permute all possible rankings, compute IFD$_{ }$ for each ranking, and identify which rankings produce the unfairest score. We find that the items can be ranked in at least one of these two ways: all relevant items at the top; or $a$ items at the top, and $|R_u^*|-a$ items at the bottom. Our simulation shows that strategy produces the unfairest score for small cut-offs $k \{1, 3\}$. As $|R_u^*|$ increases, we observe that strategy fails and strategy succeeds in achieving the unfairest score; this happens at different amounts of $|R_u^*|$, given a different number of items $n$. For example, given $n=20$ and $k=10$, strategy works for $|R_u^*| \{2, 3, , 6\}$, and for $|R_u^*| \{7, , n-k\}$ onwards, the unfairest recommendation list is in the form of , i.e., placing 6 relevant items at the top, and $|R_u^*|-6$ items at the bottom. For $|R_u^*| \{n-k+1, , n-1\}$, i.e., the general form is $|R_u^*|- = |R_u^*|-10$ items at the top, and the rest at the bottom. There are some exceptions, where this increasing pattern does not hold. For example, given $k=n=10$, for $|R_u^*| \{6,7\}$, strategy works, i.e., placing 5 items at the top and 1 item at the bottom for $|R_u^*|=6$; we denote this as 5--1. For $|R_u^*|=7$, the list is in the form of 5--2, but for $|R_u^*|=8$, the list is in the form of 4--4 instead, which deviates from the pattern. However, this deviation is unexpected as the evaluation scenario is highly unlikely , and in any case, the scores of the 4--4 and 5--3 lists only differ by $ $0.0006. To find IFD$_{ } $ , we precompute IFD$_{ } $ with the two strategies for a given $k, m |R_u^*|$, and take the maximum as IFD$_{ } $. We then compute IFD$_{ }$, which resolves the non-realisability limitation in the measure, by averaging IFD$_{ } $ across all users: _{ } = _{ } -_{ } }{_{ } - _{ } } To resolve the non-realisability in II-F, we first rewrite II-F as follows, to separate the II-F score per user: _{ } = {m} _{u U} _{ } _{} = {n} _{i I} ^2 We then min-max normalise II-F$ $: _{} = _ -_{ } }{_{ } - _{ } } To obtain II-F$_{ } $, i.e., the fairest II-F$ $, all relevant items should be ranked at the top, while to obtain II-F$_{ } $, i.e., the unfairest II-F$ $, all relevant items should be ranked at the bottom. This is because minimising the sum of squares in requires $E_{u,i}$, i.e., the exposure weight of item $i$ in user $u$'s recommendation list to be as close as possible to $E_{u,i}^*$, which is computed based on item relevance. Therefore, relevant items should be placed at the top, so that they are subtracted with nonzero exposure weights, and irrelevant items should be ranked below, such that the difference between exposure weight and $E_{u,i}^*$ is minimised. Following a similar line of reasoning, the opposite should be done to maximise the sum of squares. We also verify through simulations with $ =0.8$, $n \{10, 15, 20\}$, cut-off $k \{1, 3, 5, 10\}$, and relevant items, $|R_u^*| \{2,3, ,k-1\}$ that these ranking strategies always produce the min/max II-F$ $, or close to that . II-F, which is computed with the min-max normalised II-F$ $ , resolves the non-realisability limitation in II-F . We resolve for all measures that suffer from it and we resolve for IAA, which is the only measure that has it. For IAA$_$, the cause of and is the erroneous min-max normalisation in the linear examination function . We correct the normalisation by substituting $k$ with $k+1$ in the linear examination function , which results in: _{} = } -1}{k} = {k} This modification ensures that items at rank $ k$ receive nonzero exposure and items at $k+1$ onwards receive zero exposure. Accordingly, the IAA corrected for the non-realisability limitation needs to be computed with this corrected examination function. In the following example, we show how using the corrected examination function may result in a different score than the original examination function. For $k=2, n=4$, assume a user's recommendation list with normalised relevance values of $ $. The normalised exposure weights given by the original examination functions are $ $, while the ones given by the corrected linear examination function $_{} $ are $ $. The score of IAA$_ =0.2$ with the former, whereas IAA$_ =0.133$ with the latter. For IBO /IWO , to avoid due to division by zero, we move the divisor $Imp_i^{unif}$ in the left-hand side of the inequalities in \& to the right-hand side. We reformulate Eq.~ \& as follows: _ = {|I^-|} _{i I^-} 1.1 Imp_i^{unif} ) _ = {|I^-|} _{i I^-} 0.9 Imp_i^{unif} ) where $I^{-}$ is the set of items with at least one user that finds the item relevant. Otherwise, this results in problems in the interpretability of the scores as some items may be double counted in IBO ~and IWO . {!}{ {l|>{ }*{4}{p{3cm}}<{}} Measure & Non-realisability & Undefinedness & Zero-exposure & Top-$k$-insensitivity \\ IAA & Min-max normalising score per user& Redefining the linear examination function $e_{}$& Redefining the linear examination function $e_{}$& \\ IFD$_ $ & & & & Adding top-$k$ indicator function \\ IFD$_ $ & & & & \\ II-F & & & & \\ IBO/IWO & & Excluding items that no users will find relevant & & \\ } We have resolved some measure limitations and summarised these in . We explain why the rest are unresolvable. cannot be resolved as it is unknown what kind of recommendation list produces the fairest score . Without this formulation of the min/max score, the measures cannot be normalised to be between 0 and 1. The measures that suffer from non-realisability under Cause 1 are more complex than the ones under Cause 2; the former aggregate scores across items and tend to have more variables than the latter. As such, no explicit solution or mathematical formulation of the measures' upper and/or lower bounds has been determined. Furthermore, computationally solving for the bounds is also impractical, considering the large size of a typical RS dataset. Technically, if the theoretically achievable best and worst fairness scores are known, the non-realisability in HD, MME, IBO/IWO, and AI-F can be resolved in a similar manner to the measures in . cannot be resolved as the measures require item relevance information beyond the top-$k$ by their definitions. In previous work, to avoid having unknown item relevance information beyond the top-$k$, the model-predicted relevance is used instead of the relevance based on the test set . Consequently, the fairness score depends on how well the model estimates relevance, and will also be incomparable to that produced with other models that may have different estimations of relevance. When comparing multiple models, even if the estimated relevance from one model is used to compute the fairness score for all models, this raises the question of which model should be used as a reference. Alternatively, one can also report the possible measure range, in a similar way to RBP residuals . However, computing the measures range may be more complex than the RBP range. Unlike the RBP range, which narrows when given more document relevance labels, the measure range may widen with fewer missing labels beyond the top-$k$, due to, e.g., pairwise comparison . Thus, reporting the range of the measures can be challenging. For IFD$_{ }$, II-F, and AI-F, non-localisation can technically be resolved by modifying the measure partially. For example, if we have a fusion of IFD$_{ }$ and IFD$_{ }$, where the exposure-relevance aggregation is still done through the division operation, but for all item-pairs, instead of only pairs of relevant items, then this modification would result in a measure that does not suffer from non-localisation. For II-F and AI-F, the `target exposure' can be changed to another target that does not involve the number of relevant items for a user. However, all these approaches are substantial changes beyond simple corrections, which may fundamentally alter the behaviour or the concept behind the measure. We study the original measures and compare them to our corrected versions , which resolve some limitations of the original measures . We also investigate the robustness of the original measures by analysing the extent of the limitations that they suffer from. Here, we provide our general experimental setup, and we further describe our experiments in .{https://github.com/theresiavr/relevance-aware-item-fairness-measures-recsys}.} *{Datasets} We use four real-world datasets with varying sizes from several domains: music , e-commerce , videos , and movies . All of the datasets are as provided by ~, except for QK-video, which is obtained from . QK-video has several interaction types; we only use the `sharing' interactions. {lrrrr} dataset & {l}{\#users } & {l}{\#items } & {l}{\#interactions} & {l}{sparsity (\ Lastfm & 1,859 & 2,823 & 71,355 & 98.64 \\ Amazon-lb & 1,054 & 791 & 12,397 & 98.51 \\ QK-video & 4,656 & 6,423 & 51,777 & 99.83 \\ ML-10M & 49,378 & 9,821 & 5,362,685 & 98.89 \\ *{Preprocessing} We retain users and items with at least 5 interactions . To handle duplicate interactions, we keep the most recent one. For Amazon-lb and ML-10M, we convert ratings equal/above 3 to 1 and discard the rest, as their ratings range between $ $ and $ $, respectively. Lastfm and QK-video are not converted as they only have implicit feedback. Tab.~ presents statistics of the preprocessed datasets. *{Data Splits} To form the train/val/test sets, we apply global temporal splits with a ratio of 6:2:2 on the preprocessed Amazon-lb and ML-10M . As Lastfm and QK-video have no timestamps, we use global random splits with the same ratio. From all splits, we remove users with less than 5 interactions in the train set. *{Recommenders} We rank items with four well-known top-$k$ recommenders: item-based K-Nearest Neighbour , Bayesian Personalised Ranking , Variational Autoencoder with multinomial likelihood , and Neighbourhood-enriched Contrastive Learning . BPR, MultiVAE, and NCL are trained for 300 epochs with early stopping using RecBole . The final model is the model with the best Normalised Discounted Cumulative Gain at 10 on the validation split. To generate recommendations during testing, we exclude each user's train/val items, and rank the rest of the items. *{Fair Re-rankers} The recommenders above are not trained to optimise for fairness, so the items are re-ranked to make the recommendations fairer. We re-rank the top-$k'$ items to increase the exposure of items that are originally ranked beyond the top-$k$. We set $k'$ to be greater than the cut-off $k=10$. However, to maintain recommendation relevance, $k'$ should not be overly big as a typical RS dataset has very few relevant items per user. For all datasets and recommenders, we set $k'=25$. We re-rank items per user with COMBMNZ , a robust rank fusion method., but true similarity is hard to obtain .} CM creates a new ranking for each user by fusing two lists of scores: one based on relevance and another based on exposure. We min-max normalise the predicted relevance score to get the relevance-based score. We create the fairness-based scores from the coverage of each top-$k'$ item, i.e., based on how many times the item appears in the top-$k$. We then compute 1 minus the normalised coverage to assign a higher score for less exposed items, thus increasing fairness. After the two lists of scores are fused, they are sorted to generate the final ranking that considers both item relevance and item fairness. *{Measures} We evaluate the models with all relevance-aware fairness measures presented in and their corrected versions if they exist . For IAA, we use the ground truth relevance as item relevance. For IFD$_{ }$, we define IFD$_{ } =0$ if $u$ only has 1 relevant item in the test split. We set $ =0.9$ for HD as per while $ =0.8$ for II-F and AI-F, following . We rescale IBO/IWO into to be consistent with other measures' scale. For comparison to the measures, we evaluate recommendation effectiveness with: Hit Rate , Mean Reciprocal Rank , Precision , Recall , Mean Average Precision , and NDCG. We also evaluate fairness with measures that consider only item exposure : Jain Index , Qualification Fairness , Entropy , Fraction of Satisfied Items , and Gini Index . We compute the corrected measures as per ~. We compute all measures at $k=10$, unless stated otherwise. We evaluate recommendation performance with all , , and measures and study their correlations . We compare the achievable scores of the original measures and our corrections . We analyse their sensitivity to various cut-offs , varying number of missing relevance labels , and different recommendation effectiveness and exposure distribution . We compute all and measures , as well as both the original and corrected measures. We present all scores per dataset and model in . We denote higher-is-better measures with $ $ and vice versa with $ $. Often, the purpose of evaluation is to identify the best model in terms of effectiveness, fairness, or other aspects, so that one can choose which model to deploy. However, these aspects can be measured by one or more measures, and they may or may not reach the same conclusion regarding the best model. Here, we study if the measures agree on the best model and we further analyse the measure agreement when they rank recommender models in . We also analyse the score range as an indicator of the measure expressiveness. *{Best model agreement} Do the measures agree on the same best model? We answer this question by comparing the agreement on the best model: between and measures; among the measures; as well as between the original and corrected measures. Generally, all measures agree on the best model across all four datasets, and likewise for the measures. In contrast, the measures tend to disagree on the best model more often than the single-aspect measures. For each dataset, we observe the following. First, the best model according to measures for each dataset always differs from the one given by measures, except for QF in Amazon-lb. This means that the most effective model is generally not the fairest. Second, even though the best model per dataset is always the same for all measures and likewise for measures , this is not the case for the measures. Some measures always have the same best model per dataset , but the trend is less consistent for IFD, which only agrees with MME/AI-F occasionally for some datasets/measure versions. The measures that have the same best models tend to have similar formulations: both IBO/IWO are the percentage of items with an impact score higher/lower than a threshold; MME/AI-F aggregate exposure across users prior to taking the difference in exposure, while IAA/HD/II-F do not; and MME/IFD are pairwise measures. Third, the original IAA/II-F/IFD$_ $ always agree with their corrected versions., we verify this from the pre-rounded scores.} Yet, the original IFD$_ $ mostly disagrees with its correction. This disagreement may be because IFD$_{ }$ assigns nonzero exposure weight for items beyond top-$k$, while IFD$_{ }$ does not. In IAA/II-F/IFD$_ $ the exposure weight assignment does not differ significantly between the original and corrected version. In summary, we can cluster all measures into three groups based on their general alignment to single-aspect measures: -aligned measures, IAA/HD/II-F -aligned measures, IFD/MME/AI-F; and measures without consistent alignment, IBO/IWO. The measure alignment to single-aspect measure can somewhat be explained through their formulation. For example, the fairest ranking based on IAA and II-F is the one that places all relevant items at the top, which aligns with measures. As for AI-F, the measure scores fairer when there are more unique items exposed to the users, which aligns with measures. We also find that most original measures have the same best models as the corrected versions, except for IFD$_ $. Therefore, computing IFD may lead to a different conclusion from IFD. We revisit the measure agreement in . *{Score range} We find three issues with the range of the original measure scores: consistently small scores for several measures; scale mismatch between single-aspect measures and measures; and scale mismatch between the original measures. Regarding , the extremely low scores of the original measure scores for all datasets and models, make it hard to distinguish the performance of models for a specific dataset. For instance, IFD$_{ }$ always scores near 0 for all datasets, as is often 0 due to the low number of relevant items per user. For the same reason, MME ~ and II-F /AI-F , and often result in 0. These low scores may also be due to the limitation, as the measures cannot reach the unfairest score of 1. Our versions of IFD$_{ }$ and II-F do not suffer from this issue, as they have been normalised based on the achievable max/min score. Therefore, distinguishing the IFD and II-F scores across models for a dataset is easier than doing so for the original measures. Regarding , i.e., the scale mismatch between single-aspect measures and measures, while the above scores differ in the fourth decimal point or later, the and scores differ in the second decimal point or before. For instance, the NDCG of MultiVAE-CM and NCL for Lastfm differs by $ $0.16 and their Jain differs by $ $0.14. These differences are non-negligible, but the joint scores of IAA /IFD$_{ }$/MME /II-F /AI-F ~ differ only by $ 10^{-3}$, which may seem negligible. As the difference of magnitude is inconsistent between the single-aspect and measures, the scores are harder to interpret. On the other hand, the differences in the scores of IAA /IFD$_{ }$/II-F ~ per dataset can either be seen in the first or second decimal point, except for IFD$_{ }$, which differs in the third decimal point for Amazon-lb and QK-video. Hence, our version of IAA/IFD/II-F is more expressive than the original version, as it better matches the change in the single-aspect measures. Regarding , i.e., the scale mismatch between the original measures, we observe considerable gaps in the range of all measure scores, e.g., between IWO , HD , and AI-F , despite all three being lower-is-better measures. For instance, in ML-10M, IWO ~ $ 1$ based on its theoretical $ $-range, HD ~ is roughly a quarter of the IWO ~ score , and AI-F ~ $ 0$ . This discrepancy causes confusion in score interpretation. Between some of the original and the reformulated measures, we also find huge differences. For example, across all models for ML-10M: IAA ~ $ 0.010$ , while IAA ~ $ 0.9$ . Similarly, II-F ~ $ 0.000$ , while II-F ~ $ 0.9$ . Similar gaps can also be observed for the same measure pairs in the other datasets. Thus, evaluation with IAA ~ or II-F ~ scores may result in a misleading conclusion of having extremely fair models, even if the models are actually very unfair. In contrast, evaluating with IAA or II-F leads to a more accurate conclusion. *{Undefined scores} The scores of IBO /IWO ~ are undefined for all datasets and all models. Due to division by zero, the computation results in `nan', which stands for `not a number'. The measures are incomputable as some items are not relevant to any users, which causes to be 0, and this is a manifestation of the limitation . Our reformulation of both measures resolves this issue. {chapters/4_SIGIR24-ext/img/selected_rectangular_corr_plot_grid.pdf} , , and measures. Asterisk denotes a statistically significant correlation after applying Bonferroni's correction.} When comparing evaluation measures, it is important to understand the extent to which they agree in ranking recommender models based on the measure scores. To this end, we compute Kendall's $ $ correlation as a measure of agreement between the orderings of the recommenders based on the measure scores computed in . We also report the statistical significance of the correlations and apply Bonferroni's correction to account for multiple testing errors within a dataset. We present the result in . We study three types of agreement: between measures and single-aspect measures; among measures in general; and between each original measure and its corrected version. *{ Agreement of joint and single-aspect measures} measures are known to not correlate strongly with measures , so we do not expect the measures to correlate strongly with either or measures, as they should account for both aspects. Generally, we find that measures do not correlate consistently with measures. IBO/IWO's correlations largely vary ; IAA, HD, and II-F have positive correlations that are either moderate or strong ; IFD and MME have negative correlations that range from weak to strong ; and AI-F has non-positive correlations . We also find that the measures do not correlate consistently with measures either. Again, the correlations of IBO/IWO have large variance, though less than with measures. IAA/HD/II-F shows two distinct trends across datasets: they have negative correlations that are moderate to strong for Lastfm and QK-video, but correlate weakly for Amazon-lb and ML-10M . The correlations of IFD also vary across datasets. On one hand, IFD strongly correlates with measures for Lastfm and QK-video , $ $, and on the other hand, IFD weakly correlates to measures for the other datasets . For all datasets, MME and AI-F strongly correlate with measures, except for QF in Lastfm . In summary, even though we expect the measures to have weak correlations with both and measures as the measures consider both item relevance and exposure, this is only true for IBO/IWO. Note that measures with strong agreement to measures do not always strongly disagree with measures, and vice versa. For instance, IAA/HD/II-F strongly agree with measures for Amazon-lb, but their correlations with measures are weak. *{ Agreement among measures.} We expect the agreement among measures to follow the groupings found in . Overall, the three measure groups identified in indeed have strong positive correlations between measures of the same group and strong negative correlations between measures from different groups. For instance, IBO consistently correlates perfectly with IWO, as their formulation is similar. IAA, HD, and II-F strongly agree with each other, $ $. IFD$_{ }$ correlates moderately or highly with IFD$_{ }$, $ $ due to their similar formulations. MME and AI-F always correlate highly, $ $. IFD occasionally has moderate-to-strong correlations with MME and AI-F, $ $ for Lastfm and QK-video, but the correlations are weaker for Amazon-lb and ML-10M, $ $. Conversely, IAA/HD/II-F have moderate-to-strong disagreement with IFD, $ $ except for IFD$_{ }$ in Amazon-lb . From the findings of and , we conclude that: IBO/IWO has inconsistent relationships with single-aspect and measures; IAA/HD/II-F strongly disagrees with measures; and IFD/MME/AI-F strongly disagrees with measures . Among the measures, IBO/IWO has weak correlations with the single-aspect measures for QK-video, and similarly with IFD$_{ }$ for Amazon-lb, but the trend is inconsistent. Thus, we argue that no measures reliably account for both recommendation effectiveness and fairness, where fairness is quantified purely based on exposure. *{ Agreement between original and corrected measures} We expect each original measure to strongly correlate with its respective corrected version, as most corrections are linear transformations. As the corrected measures min-max normalises the original score per user prior to averaging across users, the original measures may not perfectly correlate to their corrections. In general, the original measures strongly agree with the corrected ones , and the correlations tend to be weaker in Amazon-lb. We cannot compare IBO /IWO ~with IBO /IWO ~ as the original version returns undefined scores for all datasets and models . IAA , IFD$_{ }$, and II-F ~perfectly correlate with their respective corrected versions for all datasets other than Amazon-lb, where $ $. IFD$_{ }$ have weaker correlations to IFD$_{ }$ . We posit that this is because correcting the top-$k$-insensitivity of IFD$_{ }$ results in a non-linear transformation of the combined exposure-relevance score in . In conclusion, while the original measures strongly agree with the corrected ones, they do not always give identical rank orderings of the models. This means that in some cases, by computing the original measure, we may reach different conclusions on the relative performances of the model as opposed to computing the corrected version. Given the fairest possible ranking, to what extent can the measures achieve their theoretical fairest or unfairest score for different datasets and different cut-off $k$? Here, we investigate: the severity of the limitation in the original measures; and the extent to which the corrected measures resolve the limitation. As such, we experiment only with the measures for which we have resolved the limitation, i.e., IAA, IFD$_ $, IFD$_ $, and II-F, and evaluate recommendations at various cut-offs, $k \{1,3,5,10\}$.
