\\ & R-Gini & 0.836 & 0.829 & 0.829 & 0.836 & 0.808 & 0.778 & 0.768 & 0.800 & 0.805 & 0.779 & 0.774 & 0.797 & 0.822 & 0.803 & 0.799 & 0.818 \\ & NDCG-Jain & 1.081 & 1.089 & 1.107 & 1.083 & 1.065 & 1.072 & 1.078 & 1.066 & 1.072 & 1.075 & 1.086 & 1.075 & 1.068 & 1.083 & 1.097 & 1.071 \\ & NDCG-Ent & 0.971 & 0.939 & 0.955 & 0.966 & 0.871 & 0.873 & 0.883 & 0.865 & 0.876 & 0.878 & 0.891 & 0.873 & 0.898 & 0.892 & 0.906 & 0.892 \\ & NDCG-Gini & 1.121 & 1.129 & 1.144 & 1.122 & 1.097 & 1.105 & 1.110 & 1.095 & 1.102 & 1.109 & 1.118 & 1.102 & 1.105 & 1.119 & 1.132 & 1.105 \\ } *{Abstract} Individual user fairness is commonly understood as treating similar users similarly. In Recommender Systems , several evaluation measures exist for quantifying individual user fairness. These measures evaluate fairness via either: the disparity in user effectiveness scores regardless of user similarity, or the disparity in items recommended to similar users regardless of item relevance. Both disparity in recommendation effectiveness and user similarity are very important in fairness, yet no existing individual user fairness measure simultaneously accounts for both of them. In brief, current user fairness evaluation measures implement a largely incomplete definition of fairness. To fill this gap, we present Pairwise User unFairness , a novel evaluation measure of individual user fairness that considers both effectiveness disparity and user similarity. Our measure works as follows. If two users get recommendations of similar NDCG, for instance, PUF will give a different fairness score depending on how similar the users are to each other, in terms of their past interactions: the case where similar users get recommendations of similar NDCG is fairer than the case where similar users get recommendations of different NDCG. PUF is the only measure that can express this important distinction. We empirically validate that PUF does this consistently across 4 datasets and 7 rankers, and robustly when varying user similarity or effectiveness. In contrast, all other measures are either almost insensitive to effectiveness disparity or completely insensitive to user similarity. We contribute the first RS evaluation measure to reliably capture both user similarity and effectiveness in individual user fairness. The evaluation of Recommender Systems has always relied heavily on effectiveness as it directly affects user utility and satisfaction. Unsurprisingly, existing work on RS fairness evaluation often uses measures that depend on effectiveness scores, especially when it comes to individual user fairness. is traditionally defined as treating similar individuals in a similar manner . We focus on attribute-free fairness for individual users, where we assume no user attribute is available other than the user identifiers and interactions . Sensitive attributes are frequently unavailable due to privacy or data incompleteness. To measure individual user fairness in RSs, the disparity in recommendation effectiveness across users is often used as a proxy of how similar the recommendation algorithm treats the users. Recommendations across users are deemed fair if similar users have similar effectiveness scores. Yet, no existing individual user fairness measure considers both effectiveness and user similarity . User similarity and recommendation effectiveness are both important, as the former affects our expectation of how close effectiveness scores should be. For example, given two users whose past interactions are highly similar, the recommendations they receive are deemed fair if their effectiveness is similar, and unfair if the effectiveness differs a lot. This is because fairness means similar users should get similar treatment. However, two dissimilar users cannot expect to get similar treatment, as their past interactions may differ, e.g., in terms of amount, frequency, or item type. Current individual user fairness measures can be grouped into those that consider: only effectiveness disparity; and only user similarity and recommendation similarity. For , different effectiveness for users is not always unfair. Some users may have only a few past interactions, and others may have very specific tastes for whom only a few items are relevant. Fairness should consider that these users are different and cannot be compared to users who, e.g., consume only popular items. For , recommending similar items to similar users may not be fair if one user is satisfied with their recommendation, but another is not. In this case, the recommendation is not really fair as its effectiveness differs. {!}{ {llcc} Measure & Reference & Effectiveness & User Similarity \\ Standard deviation & & $ $ & -\\ Gini index & & $ $ & - \\ Envy-based && $ $ & -\\ UF & &- & $ $ \\ PUF & ours & $ $ & $ $ \\ } To counter the above limitations in current measures, we propose a novel evaluation measure for individual user fairness in RS: Pairwise User unFairness . PUF quantifies individual user fairness based on the disparity in recommendation relevance between user pairs, weighted by the similarity of user pairs. As such, PUF is aligned with the definition of individual fairness and also accounts for recommendation effectiveness, and thus, user utility. Our experiments show that PUF is superior to the current measures in terms of sensitivity to changes in effectiveness scores and user similarity distribution. Overall, we contribute a new evaluation measure for individual user fairness, which considers both user similarity and disparity in recommendation effectiveness, and which does not have the same limitations as existing measures. We present the definition of individual user fairness and existing evaluation measures of individual user fairness . We define individual user fairness in RSs as per : let $u$ and $u'$ be the representations of two users; $L_u$ and $L_{u'}$ be the recommendation lists of these two users; and $M $ be a function that maps a recommendation list to a score, e.g., its effectiveness score. Any two users whose profile distance is $d $ should receive recommendations such that recommendation effectiveness satisfies $D , M ) d $, where $D$ is a distance measure. In other words, fairness is achieved when the difference in the users' recommendation effectiveness is at most $d $. This definition agrees with the definitions of RS individual user fairness in . We present existing evaluation measures of attribute-free individual user fairness in RSs . To our knowledge, these are all such measures for RSs published up to January 2025 . All these measures quantify unfairness, so that the lower their scores, the fairer . Some measures are based on general-purpose inequality measures, i.e., standard deviation and Gini Index ; others are based on unique fairness concepts, i.e., envy-freeness and distance-based . All existing measures except the distance-based measure quantify effectiveness disparity but ignore user similarity. While the existing distance-based measure considers user similarity, it is detached from effectiveness. Hence, no existing individual user fairness measure in RS considers both user similarity and effectiveness disparity. $ $SD measures how dispersed the data is in relation to the mean. In RSs, SD and variance are often used to quantify individual user fairness. RS is fair if it provides equal prediction accuracy to all users, and fairness is evaluated via the variance of the mean squared error of user rating prediction . Other works measure fairness from user recommendation lists, e.g., via the variance of NDCG scores across users or the SD of user utility . The utility of user $u$, when given the top $k$ recommendation list of user $u'$, $L_{u'}$ is originally defined as follows: ^{ori}_u = } _{u,i} }{ _{i R^k_{u'}} _{u,i}} where $_{u,i}$ is the predicted relevance score of user $u$ for item $i$, and $R_u^k$ is the top-$k$ most relevant items of $u$ based on the ground truth. Two issues arise from the above formulation: computing user utility based on predicted relevance $_{u,i}$ may not reflect the true recommendation effectiveness; and relevance ties for items in $R_u^k$ are not handled, leading to multiple possible compositions of $R_u^k$. Further, in , the predicted relevance score is also taken from a latent factorisation model, which means that recommending items based on decreasing predicted relevance , will always result in $ ^{ori}_u =1$ for all users, rendering the user utility measure pointless. We resolve the above issues in the utility function, by replacing predicted relevance with ground truth relevance $r_{u,i}$, as follows: _u = } r_{u,i} }{ _{i R^k_{u'}} r_{u,i}} For binary relevance, $r_{u,i} \{0,1\}$, assuming that each user has at least $k$ relevant items, $ _u ={k} _{i L_{u}} 1_{\{i R_u\}}$ or simply Precision@$k$ . The indicator function $1_{\{ \}}$ returns 1 if the expression $ $ is true, and 0 otherwise. We denote the set of user $u$'s relevant items as $R_u$. As such, the SD based on the reformulated utility function is SD-P $= SD_{u U} _u $, where $U$ is the set of users. $ $Gini is a well-known inequality measure that quantifies the extent to which a distribution deviates from a perfectly equal distribution. It has been used to measure individual user fairness from different distributions, e.g., P@$k$ , NDCG , F1, or the utility score per user . Fairness has been measured as envy-freeness. Envy is defined as the extra utility a user $u$ would have received if they were given the recommendation list of user $u'$, $L_{u'}$ . Formally, the envy of user $u$ towards $u'$ is computed as:) is used to compute envy in . WLOG, user utility is computed slightly differently in as the work is on online learning.} envy = )- _u , 0 \}} Three fairness evaluation measures are based on this concept, where envy is aggregated differently: Mean Envy , Mean Max Envy , and Proportion of $ $-Envious User : ME = {m } _{u U} _{} envy MME = {m} _{u U} _{u' U}{ \{ envy \} } PEU = {m} _{u U} 1_{\{ _{u' U}{ envy > } \}} where $m=|U|$ is the number of users, and $ $ is the envy tolerance hyperparameter. A user $u$ is said to be $ $-envious of user $u'$ if their gain in user utility exceeds $ $. In , fairness for individual users is defined as any two similar users $u,u'$ receiving similar recommendations. Recommendation disparity is then measured with UnFairness score . UF uses both the user similarity, $sim_{UF} $, and the pairwise distance between the representation of items in the recommendation list of user $u$ and of user $u'$, $d_L $. User similarity is modelled by both Jaccard of the user's set of past interactions $H_u$ and JS-div between item feature distributions in the interactions of user $u$ and $u'$, $D_{JS} $, where $ $ is the item feature. UF is computed as: UF = sim_{UF} d_L } sim_{UF} = \ sim_{Jacc} + \ sim_{JS} d_L = {k^2} _{i L_{u}} _{i' L_{u'}} d_{cos} sim_{Jacc} = |}{|H_u H_{u'}|} sim_{JS} = 1-D_{JS} where $U^*$ is the set of similar user pairs with $sim_{UF} \ t$, and $t$ is the similarity threshold. In , $t=mean \ + SD $, where the mean and SD are computed for all user pairs. We denote as $ $ the weight for $sim_{Jacc}$, as $k$ the recommendation cut-off, and as $d_{cos} $ the cosine distance between the representations of items $i$ and $i'$. The log base in is unclear. Note that UF does not consider recommendation effectiveness. We present our evaluation measure of individual user fairness, Pairwise User unFairness . PUF consists of two components: the similarity between users and the disparity in recommendation effectiveness. Next, we describe: how user similarity is computed; how to quantify the disparity of recommendation effectiveness, while taking into account user similarities. Measuring similarity between users is an inherently hard problem and there is no single ground truth of what makes two users similar . When there is no user attribute, as per our focus on attribute-free fairness , user profiles can be modelled based on their historical interactions . Similarity between users is then computed pairwise based on the user representation , for example, with cosine similarity or Jaccard . PUF quantifies individual user fairness based on disparity in recommendation effectiveness, considering user similarity. PUF measures the mean pairwise difference in the effectiveness score per user, weighted by the user pair similarity. Based on and , we define PUF as follows: = {m } _{u U} _{} sim |S - S | where $U$ is the set of all users , $sim $ is the similarity of users $u$ and $u'$, $S$ is an effectiveness measure, e.g., P@$k$. $S$ must range or scaled to be in $ $, so PUF also ranges in $ $. PUF is modular; it can be operated with any similarity or effectiveness measure as long as it fulfils the range requirement. While both PUF and UF consider user similarity, PUF differs from UF as PUF considers the difference in the recommendation relevance, rather than only the disparity based on the representation of the recommended items as in UF. Moreover, recommending different sets of items to two similar users may be considered unfair by UF even if both users like their recommendations, but it is fair based on PUF. In theory, users with similar tastes in the past are likely to have a similar preference in the future . Yet, the recommendation problem is challenging as even two highly similar users may not equally like their recommended items, if they are given identical items. This may be due to, for example, incomplete historical data that is unrepresentative of user taste, diverging user preference, or limited ground truth data. These limitations necessitate a look into the disparity of the recommendation relevance, which is what our measure quantifies. Overall, our measure, PUF, aligns with the definition of individual user fairness and quantifies fairness through the disparity in recommendation effectiveness, which is more meaningful than the disparity in the representation of recommended items, as effectiveness relates more to user utility. We use 7 well-known collaborative filtering recommenders that represent different model types: user- and item-based $K$-nearest neighbour , Bayesian Personalised Ranking , Variational Autoencoder with multinomial likelihood , Neural Graph Collaborative Filtering , Neural Matrix Factorisation , and Neighbourhood-enriched Contrastive Learning . All models except U- and I-KNN are trained for 300 epochs with early stopping. We tune hyperparameters with grid search. The configuration with the best NDCG@10 during validation is the final model. A measure that does not exhibit a wide range of scores across various fairness levels is less useful in distinguishing changes in fairness. Such measures may create an illusion of a negligible difference in fairness, due to their compressed empirical range . Across datasets, the observed range of each measure varies, except for MME which is consistently extremely small . Changing a user's recommendation list with another user's as in does not generally result in a large increase in the user's P@$k$ , which translates to low MME. ME and PEU are unaffected by this issue even if they are based on , as ME accounts for envy across all users rather than the maximum envy per user, and PEU employs an envy threshold. In short, MME is the least sensitive as it fails to discriminate between models for the same and across datasets, while other measures, including PUF, are more sensitive. The most sensitive measures, i.e., the ones with the widest observed range, are Gini and PEU. . We report the average computation time of the measures in . All runs are done with AMD EPYC 75F3 for a fair comparison. User pairwise similarity is computed once per dataset for each PUF variant. We find that half of the existing measures, i.e., ME, MME, PEU, and UF are computationally expensive , while PUF is significantly faster , despite also being a pairwise measure. ME/MME/PEU/UF need additional extensive computation per pair, which makes them expensive: UF does nested pairwise comparison in and , while ME/MME/PEU recompute the effectiveness score for each user pair . In short, PUF is computationally more efficient and thus more practical to compute than most existing measures. {chapters/6_PUF/img/selected_rectangular_corr_plot.pdf} measures, existing measures, and PUFs for all recommenders.} An important aspect when comparing evaluation measures is how much they agree when their scores are used to rank models from best to worst. If one measure can be used to estimate the rank ordering given by another, there is no point in using both measures if we are only interested in ranking models. To study this, we compute Kendall's $ $ correlation for all and measures. Kendall's $ $ can handle ties and is more robust to nonlinear relationships . If two measures have $ 0.9$, we consider their rankings equivalent . shows the agreement between and measures, and among measures. and measures}. Across datasets, the agreement between and measures varies from strong disagreement to moderate-to-strong agreement . Our PUF consistently disagrees with measures, even if the disagreement is weaker for Lastfm, $ $. As no measure consistently has $| | 0.9$ with any measure for all datasets, their model orderings cannot be precisely inferred from that of measures. measures}. Regarding the agreement between PUF and existing measures, we find that only SD aligns with PUF , while the rest, including UF, tend to disagree or have a weak correlation with PUF, $ $. Both UF and PUF consider user similarity, yet UF weakly correlates to PUF for all datasets except QK-video . This weak relationship may be due to UF not considering item relevance, while PUF does. In contrast, despite not being similarity-based, SD correlates the strongest to PUF. Yet, most of its rankings are not equivalent to PUF , which means that fairness evaluation with SD can lead to a conclusion that misaligns with PUF. Even if we only compare the best model based on the measures instead of the model rankings, UF and PUF never agree . Thus, using UF and PUF to gauge fairness may differ in the conclusion on which model is the fairest. Overall, we find that existing measures frequently disagree on model orderings with PUF, regardless of whether the measure accounts for user similarity. While SD has the most similar conclusions to PUF, it still does not give equivalent rankings to PUF. {chapters/6_PUF/img/artificial_rel_QK_ML20.pdf} ) and fairness scores of QK-video and ML-20M, when artificially varying \ } It is important that a fairness measure can capture changes across differences in the recommendation quality across users, as fairness is related to the disparity in recommendation . Thus, any change in scores should also be reflected in the scores. To this end, we study how varying item relevance affects the and measures. All measure equations except for UF explicitly account for effectiveness, but it is unknown how sensitive measures are to changes in scores. The change of relevance scores is artificially done as follows. For all users, we start by recommending $k$ relevant items . For users with more than $k$ relevant items, we select the relevant items randomly. For users with fewer than $k$ relevant items, we fill the remaining slots with random irrelevant items to ensure that each user receives exactly $k$ items. In each iteration, we replace the recommendation of 10\ shows the results for QK-video and ML-20M, which represent the overall trends in all our datasets; we show the rest in . We find that PUF fits the expectation of having decreasing fairness followed by increasing fairness, denoted by the inverted parabolas. This trend is more pronounced for the ML-* datasets, as the mean user pairwise similarity is higher than for the other datasets. Among existing measures, only SD follows this expectation, while the others show undesirable tendencies: as decreases, Gini and PEU significantly become less fair, and ME does the same to a minor extent. The overall decrease in fairness in these measures is undesirable, as the measure scores closely resemble decreasing effectiveness instead of the disparity in the scores distribution. Even worse, MME and UF are almost invariant to the change in recommendation effectiveness: MME tends to have extremely small scores to begin with , while UF does not depend on item relevance . To summarise, we have shown that PUF and SD quantify fairness based on the disparity in recommendation effectiveness. All other existing measures ignore the actual disparity in scores. They merely reflect decreasing effectiveness or are not sensitive to the change in effectiveness. Next, we study whether the change in user similarity distribution is reflected in the measures. [width=0.75 , trim=0.3cm 0.2cm 0.2cm 0.2cm, clip=True]{chapters/6_PUF/img/artificial_similarity_QK_ML20.pdf} $ observed in the dataset. The distribution skewness differs across datasets. } Individual user fairness is defined based on user similarity. It is important to know if and how user similarity affects fairness. While two or more recommender models should be evaluated under the same similarity distribution, a desirable property of an individual user fairness measure is the ability to distinguish a single model performance across different similarity distributions, that may arise from various ways of quantifying user similarity. To this end, we investigate how the measures respond to varying user similarity distributions, where the distribution is artificially generated. User similarity distributions tend to be right-skewed for random users, and left-skewed for users that are friends . Further, users are often dissimilar, as some users are new to the systems or do not engage much, leading to discrepancies between the number of interactions among users and potentially affecting user similarity. Considering the above, we create synthetic user similarity scores by sampling from the Weibull distribution , which can be used to model skewed distributions. It has been used to model user rating distributions and sampling user neighbour candidates in RSs . Its probability density function is $p = x^{ -1} $. To obtain various right- and left-skewed distributions to represent possible user similarity distributions, we set $ \{0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 50\}$. We also sample from the normal distribution $ $, which has zero skews . We then min-max normalise the sampled similarity values to rescale them in the $ $-range and randomly assign them to user pairs. We analyse non-random assignment in . While the user similarity is artificial, we use the actual recommendation lists and scores from the NCL model runs as they perform relatively well. To save computation time, we only compute SD to represent all similarity-independent measures; theoretically, these measures will remain constant given no change in their input. We also compute PUF and UF, the two similarity-based measures. We compare the measure scores with the scores corresponding to the user similarity distribution observed in the datasets based on $sim_{Jacc}$ . We present the results for QK-video and ML-20M in ; we show the rest in as their trends are similar. We find that the similarity-based measures PUF and UF become fairer as skewness increases. Increasing skewness means a higher proportion of user pairs with low similarity, hence $ $PUF and $ $UF tend to be lower. Conversely, but as expected, the similarity-independent SD remains constant despite the change in skewness. For highly skewed similarities , which is a realistic similarity distribution as seen in QK-video, SD-NDCG is somewhat unfair for all datasets. Yet, PUF is almost perfectly fair . Therefore, simply using SD may lead to the underestimation of fairness. While we only compute SD here, we expect other existing measures to exhibit the same invariance as they are similarity-independent. Between the two similarity-based measures, PUF is more sensitive towards negatively skewed user similarity distribution than UF. As skewness decreases, the mean user similarity increases at a slower rate. UF only considers user pairs above the mean-based similarity threshold, thus the number of user pairs contributing to the log sum in decreases slower than in the right-skewed distributions. Another concern of UF is its relatively high unfairness compared to PUF and SD, even when most users are dissimilar. This may be because UF computes the pairwise distance of the representation of the recommended items of a user pair . Minimising this distance is difficult as each $k$ recommended item of a user must have a similar representation to each of the other user's $k$ items, regardless of item relevance. In short, we show that PUF can distinguish fairness levels across various similarity distributions, while non-similarity-based measures cannot. This distinction indicates the strength of PUF over SD . We find that disregarding user similarity can also lead to the misinterpretation of fairness level. In the following part, we further compare the two similarity-based measures and highlight the strengths of PUF over UF. We compare the similarity-based measures under extreme scenarios: can their scores reflect the difference in maximum and minimum fairness, across differences in the recommendation quality? Given an artificial set of pairwise user similarities and an artificial set of pairwise score differences, to simulate the fairest case , we sort these values and assign a higher similarity value to user pairs with lower score difference. Separately, we assign a higher similarity score to pairs with higher score difference to mimic the unfairest case . For MostFair, a desirable measure would score close to 0 . For MostUnfair, it should exhibit an inverted U-shape when the score distribution is varied , as the maximum unfairness happens when the distribution is the most uneven. We use an artificial, right-skewed user similarity distribution sampled from the Weibull distribution with $ =2$ . We use the P@$k$ and NDCG@$k$ scores per user from the artificial runs in . To compute PUF- and UF-NDCG, we assign the user similarities to user pairs following the sorted pairwise difference of NDCG, and likewise for PUF- and UF-Prec. The results for QK-video and ML-20M are shown in ; the rest are shown in as we find similar trends. We see that the MostFair assignment yields PUF scores that are overall close to the fairest across varying recommendation effectiveness, but UF remains constantly unfair nonetheless. This emphasises a mismatch between fairness computed based on the disparity of item representation and based on score differences. PUF scores are more unfair for the MostUnfair than the MostFair scenario, while UF is almost invariant to the change in similarity assignment between MostFair and MostUnfair. We observe again the insensitivity of UF towards varying recommendation effectiveness, as seen in . In the MostUnfair case, $ $PUF ranges in $[0,0.2)$ for all datasets. This is because the overall user similarity is low, which can happen in real-world scenarios. Hence, the maximum unfairness is expected to be relatively low, as PUF relies on user similarity to quantify fairness. All in all, PUF correctly scores close to the fairest in the MostFair assignment, while UF fails to do so. PUF is notably more unfair for the MostUnfair rather than the MostFair case, while UF is almost constant for both cases and across varying recommendation effectiveness. In both cases, UF also overestimates effectiveness-based unfairness by constantly scoring much higher than PUF. Overall, PUF correctly quantifies maximal and minimal fairness, while UF does not. Next, we study the effect of using the same similarity measure for generating recommendations and evaluating fairness. {chapters/6_PUF/img/artificial_sim_rel_sorted_QK_ML20} We ask: if PUF and the recommendation model use the same similarity measure, would the model be the fairest according to PUF? The goal is to validate whether PUF gives an advantage to models that use the same similarity function as PUF. To answer this, we use cosine similarity for both PUF and the U-KNN model . We find that U-KNN is never the fairest according to PUF. Even though U-KNN is the second-best model based on PUF for Lastfm, it is the second worst for ML-10M and it is the unfairest for ML-20M. So, using the same similarity measure for both PUF and the recommender model does not lead to the model being the fairest based on PUF. This may be due to the data splits: let two users have identical items in the train set, but completely different items in the test set. Even if both users get the same recommendation list, their effectiveness scores may differ and PUF cannot be the fairest. Therefore, minimising PUF to optimise for pairwise user fairness while maintaining an overall high mean effectiveness scores for all users, is not a trivial problem. We next study what happens in a scenario where item features are available. PUF and UF are the only measures that can incorporate item features in their similarity functions. We compare PUF to UF when varying the importance of item features to them. We compute PUF and UF with $sim_{UF}$ and vary the weighted sum of its two components with $ \{0, 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, 1\}$. The weight $ =0$ accounts only for users' item feature distribution, while $ =1$ accounts only for their past interactions. We evaluate the groundwork runs) and find similar results .} for the ML-* datasets, with movie genres as features. We cannot use the other datasets as QK-video only has two item categories and Lastfm has none. We present the results in . Overall, we find that PUF scores are fairer with higher weights of the users' past interactions.).} Users' past interactions tend to be more diverse than their item category distributions, leading to more dissimilar users and less weight of user effectiveness disparity, which means fairer $ $PUF. UF follows the trend of PUF for ML-10M, but differs for ML-20M, where at first it becomes more unfair, then fairer with a higher $ $. The non-linear trend of UF for ML-20M is due to its self-imposed thresholding, which results in highly different numbers of similar user pairs across various $ $. While the effect of $ $ on UF can be inconsistent for different datasets, it is more predictable for PUF, which always exhibits near-linear relationship. For both datasets, UF is always more unfair than PUF, which is consistent with our prior results . For both measures, the ordering of models given for a $ $ is maintained for another, i.e., the same conclusion of which model is better than another can be reached despite the different scores for different weights. To sum up, varying the weights of interaction- and feature-based user similarity impacts PUF in a more predictable way than UF, making PUF superior to UF due to its more intuitive behaviour. {chapters/6_PUF/img/item_aware.pdf} $ , varying the weighted sum of users' past interactions and their item feature distribution with $ $. } In we overviewed existing individual user fairness measures. Next, we discuss work that studies RS fairness measures empirically and proposes pairwise individual fairness measures for ranking . Our work is close to , which studies item group fairness measures in RS, and , which studies evaluation measures of individual item fairness. Similarly to , we find that fairness measures may disagree in their model ordering, and that some measures are more sensitive than others, given decreasing effectiveness and disparity in the recommendations. Out of all individual fairness measures in , we often see that the similarity criterion of individual fairness is ignored, with only a few measures incorporating it. Recent work also proposes pairwise individual fairness measures, but these are for individual items, whereas our pairwise measure, PUF, is for individual users. Further, PUF considers user similarity, while the measure in does not. The measure in is similar to UF, as both employ thresholding of user similarities. Instead of applying a threshold, which can be arbitrary, PUF is weighted by user similarities, which introduces degrees of user similarity in the fairness computation. There exists also work on user group fairness or counterfactual fairness . Most such work requires sensitive attributes , but public recommendation datasets with sensitive attributes tend to lack user representations , and grouping users may require discretising the attribute . We focus on attribute-free individual fairness, rather than group or counterfactual fairness, to better assess distribution across all individuals . Current evaluation measures of individual user fairness in RSs either exclusively consider disparity in recommendation effectiveness or user similarity, but never both jointly. This means that none of these measures aligns with the definition of individual user fairness and one of the key objectives of RSs: user utility. To address this issue, we introduced PUF, a novel evaluation measure that quantifies user fairness through pairwise difference in effectiveness scores, while considering similarity between users. Note that accounting for both aspects is not trivial. For example, dividing the user similarity by the effectiveness disparity may result in an infinitely large score if the effectiveness disparity is small, and the score is undefined if the recommendation effectiveness is perfectly equal. We showed that PUF is empirically superior to existing measures, based on the definition of individual fairness that we adopt . Firstly, PUF is superior to non-similarity-based measures, such as SD. The non-similarity-based measures are insensitive to changes in user similarity distribution, which is a critical flaw as they cannot give a distinct fairness score for different levels of user similarity. Considering both user similarity and effectiveness disparity is crucial as failing to do so may lead to over/underestimation of fairness. Secondly, PUF is also better than UF, the only existing measure that factors in user similarity. We have shown that PUF scores correspondingly to the disparity in user utility and successfully quantifies contrasting levels of fairness, while UF fails to do both. While the PUF design is simple, it is a novel, intuitive measure that is easy to interpret and robust against various effectiveness disparity or user similarity, which sets it apart from existing metrics, addressing a crucial gap in RS fairness evaluation. In summary, we recommend using PUF over other existing individual user fairness measures due to its alignment with the definition of fairness, computational efficiency, and sensitivity to varying levels of user similarities and recommendation effectiveness in both typical and extreme cases. PUF paves the way for more elaborate measures in fairness evaluation, such as with graded relevance or other ways of quantifying user similarity . 0.0532 & 0.0581 & 0.0576 \\ & & $ $ $$ & 0.0361 & 0.0326 & 0.0333 & 0.0328 & 0.0317 & 0.0346 & 0.0343 \\ } *{Abstract} Fairness in recommender systems is commonly categorised into group fairness and individual fairness. However, there is no established scientific understanding of the relationship between the two fairness types, as prior work on both types has used different evaluation measures or evaluation objectives for each fairness type, thereby not allowing for a proper comparison of the two. As a result, it is currently not known how increasing one type of fairness may affect the other. To fill this gap, we study the relationship of group and individual fairness through a comprehensive comparison of evaluation measures that can be used for both fairness types. Our experiments with 8 RSs across 3 datasets show that recommendations that are highly fair for groups can be very unfair for individuals. Our finding is novel and useful for RS practitioners aiming to improve the fairness of their systems. Fairness in Recommender Systems can be evaluated for groups and for individuals. typically refers to having equitable outcome across groups , while is commonly defined as treating similar users/items equally . Conceptually, prior work discusses how RSs can be fair to groups and at the same time unfair to individuals, or vice versa, but no work has empirically studied how this practically occurs in RS fairness evaluation. Prior work either: evaluates fairness exclusively for groups or individuals ; or evaluates both, but with two different families of measures or for two fairness subjects/objectives . Evaluating group and individual fairness with different families of measures makes comparison difficult, as the measure scores may differ in sensitivity, or in theoretical and empirical ranges . Likewise, it is not possible to properly compare group and individual fairness when each is evaluated for a distinct fairness objective, e.g., recommendation effectiveness disparity across individual users vs.~exposure disparity between item groups . To address this gap, we evaluate user-side group and individual fairness with the same families of measures that can quantify both, to study the relationship between evaluation measures of user-side group and individual fairness. This work is the first empirical study that compares the 9 existing user-side fairness evaluation measures for groups with those for individuals. We ask: To what extent do group and individual fairness evaluation measures differ in their conclusions? For the same family of measures, how different are the group and individual fairness scores? How do different ways of grouping users affect between- and within-group fairness? How do between- and within-group fairness relate to individual fairness? Our results show that group fairness measures often hide unfairness within groups and between individuals, highlighting the importance of evaluating fairness beyond the between-group level. We compare evaluation measures of user-side individual and group fairness in RSs, considering multiple ways of grouping users..} *{Datasets} To enable group fairness analysis, three datasets with $ 3$ user profile features are selected . has 1,000,029 movie ratings from 6K users. Users with no/unspecified self-reported gender, age, or occupation are removed, and we exclude users under 18 years to avoid processing the data of minors. We focus on recommending preferred movies, so ratings <3 are discarded, and the levels 4 and 5 are mapped to 1. has 1.6M job applications from 321K users. Given a user's application history, we focus on recommending job titles that may suit them, keeping only users with information for degree, major, and years of experience. Users with more than 60 years of experience are removed . has $1{,}088{,}161{,}692$ music playcounts, from $ $120K users. We focus on recommending new track artists for a user to listen to, other than the ones they have listened to in the past, using the dataset after deduplication based on the artist, with 65M interactions . The deduplication summarises the total playcount per artist and keeps the last event timestamp. Users without countries, age, or gender information are removed, as are minors , and users with age >100 years . Items without name/title are removed from all datasets. To reduce data sparsity, which may affect LLMRec performance , we keep users and items with $ $ 5 interactions for ML-1M and JobRec. We apply 50-core filtering to LFM-1B, as it is highly sparse with 5-core filtering. The data is temporally split for train/val/test with a ratio of 3:1:1 using a global timeline . From all splits, users and items with $ t$ interactions in the train set are removed. A high $t$ can result in very few unique users in the test set, so we choose $t$ such that at least 500 test users remain. For ML-1M and LFM-1B, we set $t=5$ . For JobRec, we use $t=2$. We remove users/items in the val and test sets that are not in the train set. $ is the number of groups for sensitive attribute $a$. We exclude empty groups.} {!}{ {lrrr} & ML-1M & JobRec & LFM-1B \\ \#interaction & 467,218 & 210,921 & 15,024,267 \\ \#item & 3,030 & 19,912 & 51,204 \\ \#user & 620 & 523 & 16,611 \\ sensitive attr. \#1 & gender & degree & gender \\ sensitive attr. \#2 & age & years of experience & age \\ sensitive attr. \#3 & occupation & major & country \\ \#intersectional groups & 12 & 36 & 29 \\ min--max subgroup size & 2--279 & 1--70 & 1--3,919 \\ median subgroup size & 31 & 7 & 59 \\ } *{User Grouping} To study group fairness, we cluster users based on their sensitive attributes . Users cannot belong to two groups at the same time, e.g., age<50 and age$ $50. For ML-1M, we use gender, age, and occupation as sensitive attributes. Gender is used as is. Age is grouped into: 18--24 years, 25--49 years, and $ $50 years . User occupation is grouped into: non-working and working . For JobRec, we consider the user's academic degree, years of working experience, and study major as the sensitive attributes. Degree is grouped into: high school, college , and university . Years of experience are grouped into: $ $5 years, >5--10 years, and >10 years. We group study majors into six fields of study, as per ~, using manual annotation and fuzzy string matching..} For LFM-1B, the sensitive attributes are gender, age, and country. Gender and age are processed as for ML-1M, and the user's country is mapped to the continent., and manually group transcontinental countries.} Users from the North/South Americas are grouped together with Antarctica . *{LLM-based Recommenders} Recent work has utilised Large Language Models as recommenders , with promising results . Unlike collaborative filtering models, LLMRecs can easily handle fine-grained user attributes, although including sensitive attributes in the prompt can impact effectiveness and fairness . We therefore study the effectiveness and fairness of LLMRecs under few-shot learning. To ensure comparable performance, we use four open-source, similar-sized LLMs released in July--Nov'24: Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct , Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct , GLM-4-9B-chat , and Ministral-8B-Instruct-2410 . The temperature is fixed at 0 for each LLM to obtain deterministic output. The LLMs are prompted using in-context learning strategy , as this has been shown to outperform sequential and recency-focused prompting..} We only prompt for users that exist in the test set, as otherwise it is not possible to evaluate the recommendation effectiveness. In the prompt, we provide the users' train items as the interaction history and the val items as the few-shot samples. A maximum of 10 most recent train and val items each are provided, as having too many items in the prompt may reduce effectiveness . To avoid inflated performance, we do not prompt with a sampled candidate item list . Instead, we add restriction in the prompt to narrow down the item search space, e.g., for ML-1M, the movies should be between certain years, based on the movie release year in the metadata file. For LFM-1B, the prompt also includes the playcount, which is important in music recommendation . Based on the inclusion/exclusion of user sensitive attributes, we create two prompt types : Sensitive , which has both interaction history and all three sensitive attributes, and Non-Sensitive , which has only the interaction history. To evaluate LLMRecs, we perform string matching between the list of recommended items and items in the test set, by using the TF-IDF of the items' character-based n-gram . The recommended item is deemed relevant if similarity $ 0.75$. Our LLMRecs experiments are carried out with and . *{Evaluation} Recommendation effectiveness and fairness are measured at $k=10$ for all LLMRecs. For , the mean Hit Rate , MRR, P@$k$ , and NDCG@$k$ are computed over all users. Group and individual measures are computed in two steps: first, computing an score per user/group as a `base score'; and second, aggregating the `base score' between users/groups with a measure. P and NDCG are used as base scores to represent set- and rank-based measures. We compute all existing fairness measures for two or more user groups in RSs, that are published up to March 2025: Average scores of the worst 25\ MAD , Gini , CV , FStat , KL , and GCE . We also compute the Atkinson Index , an income inequality measure that considers within-group variations..} The between- and within-group fairness version of the measures are denoted as $ _$ and $ _$ respectively. refers to between-group fairness; the latter is used when we compare fairness between and within groups.} We provide the measure formulations and technical details in . Fairness for individual users is quantified with SD , Gini , and Atk. The subscript $ $ indicates the individual fairness version of the measure. SD and Gini are the only measures that have been used for both individual and group user fairness, while Atk can be decomposed into between- and within-group fairness with no residuals . While other group measures can also be used to measure individual fairness, their scores may not be informative, e.g., Min may be zero for most models, as having most users scoring P=0 or NDCG=0 is common. *{Evaluation of all LLMRecs} ) and fairness scores at cut-off $k=10$ for intersectional groups ~and for individuals ~of LLMRecs with non-sensitive and sensitive prompts. This could be because the job titles are very specific and contain more noise than movie/artist names . Generally, as group/individual measures, Gini, Atk, and CV are fairer when NDCG is better; this is because their equations include division by the mean NDCG. As such, these measures can distinguish group fairness between two models that have similar SD$_$/SD$_$ but differ in NDCG , or vice versa. . The and scores between sensitive and non-sensitive prompts are generally comparable, implying that including sensitive attributes in the prompt has little effect on effectiveness/fairness. An exception to this is Ministral-8B for LFM-1B, which may be due to the LLM's relatively high gender bias . . For Atk/Gini, the fairest model for intersectional groups and for individual users are always the same, but this is not the case for SD. Thus, if sensitive attribute information is missing, Atk$_$/Gini$_$ can estimate which model is the fairest for intersectional groups. *{Agreement of group and individual fairness measures} {chapters/7_Intersectional/img/corr_ind-group_LLM.pdf} and group in ranking LLMRecs. User groups are based on 3 sensitive attributes. Due to 8-way ties, $ $ cannot be computed for Min . } Do individual and group measures reach the same conclusion? If an individual measure can rank models from the most to the least fair equivalently to a group measure, one can be a proxy for the other. To assess measure agreement in ranking models, we compute Kendall's $ $; we consider two rankings to be ``equivalent'' if $ .9$ . shows group and individual measure agreement. Our results show that measure consistently has equivalent rankings to any group measures}.$ vs SD$_$) for all possible groupings .} For all datasets, group measures tend to have weak-to-strong agreement with Gini/Atk and have weak-to-strong disagreement with SD. The only group measure that always correlates strongly to an individual measure is CV . This may be due to the measures' similar formulation: Gini /Atk have division by the mean scores across all users, while CV has division by the mean of mean score per group. measures make a reliable proxy for group measures, hence the need to evaluate for both.} *{Intersectional fairness} As we consider more intersectional attributes to form user groups, the number of groups grows. We posit that achieving intersectional group fairness is harder than for non-intersectional groups as it requires having similar scores for a larger number of groups. Likewise, individual fairness would be worse than group fairness. We study: if/how the number of attributes used for grouping affect fairness; and the difference between individual and group scores. To this end, we compute NDCG-based individual scores across all users, and average NDCG-based group scores across all ways of grouping users when considering only 1, 2, or 3 attribute at once. SD, Gini, and Atk are computed, as they have been used as both group and individual measures. We evaluate the runs from GLM-4-9B due to their relatively good NDCG and scores across all datasets. Regarding , we find that . For all measures and datasets, the individual scores are always worse than group scores. These findings imply that group scores may mask unfairness within groups, even if the within-group variation is considered . {chapters/7_Intersectional/img/different_ways_of_grouping_between_LLM.pdf} scores of GLM-4-9B . Grp is the mean scores across all ways of grouping users when considering $a$ attribute . For $a \{1,2\}$, the \# of ways to group users is three each. For $a=3$, there is only one way to group users. } {chapters/7_Intersectional/img/decomposability_all_JobRec.pdf} {chapters/7_Intersectional/img/decomposability_all_LFM-1B.pdf} *{Fairness decomposability} When fairness is evaluated only between groups, within-group unfairness may occur undetected. As the number of groups increases, we analyse: how between- and within- group fairness change; and how they relate to individual fairness. To this end, we compute between- and within-group fairness, as well as individual fairness with SD, Gini, and Atk on the NDCG scores from GLM-4-9B . Results for JobRec and LFM-1B are shown in ; we find similar trends for ML-1M . Regarding , we find that while between-group fairness generally worsens as the number of groups grows, within-group fairness tends to remain stable, except for SD of JobRec, where it worsens. Regarding , we see that within-group unfairness is almost as high as individual unfairness, and always higher than between-group unfairness. Sometimes, within-group unfairness even exceeds individual unfairness . In summary, { a single RS can vary in group fairness for different ways of grouping users, while being highly unfair for users within groups and for individual users at the same time.} We empirically studied the relationship between group and individual fairness in RSs. We found that RSs which are fair for groups can still be very unfair for individual users, providing the first empirical evidence on the disjointness of these two RS fairness concepts. Overall, we encourage evaluating individual and within-group fairness alongside group fairness, as between-group scores may mask huge disparity in RS effectiveness across users, even for a large number of groups (i.e., 5\ For JobRec, we explain in more detail how we group users based on the study major. The study major is in free text format, so we first performed exploratory data analysis to get a better overview of the its distribution. More than 80\ We summarise the statistics of user sensitive attributes in . We present the size of intersectional groups for each dataset in {tab:intersectional_group_LFM}. We provide the templates and examples for the non-sensitive prompts in and for the sensitive prompts in . In the S-prompt, we replace ``a user'' in the NS-prompt with a description containing their sensitive attribute, e.g., ``a user with a bachelor's degree, majoring in biology, has a total of 10 years of experience'. We use the original user attribute prior to re-grouping as they are more informative and precise . While this may not be the most natural way a user may interact with LLMs , it is a way to ensure that the LLMs have information on the users' sensitive attribute. To enhance reproducibility, we provide the formulations of all measures that we compute. A measure can be used to compute individual fairness, group fairness, and/or within-group fairness. We denote the individual, between-group, and within-group fairness versions of a measure as $ _$, $ _$, and $ _$, respectively. When applicable, we provide the technical details that we used. $ $/$ $ means the higher/lower the better measures fairness between groups. To compute Min, the score is first calculated per user, and then averaged per user group. The group mean score is then sorted, and the first quartile value is set as a threshold. Min averages the mean group score of groups with an score below/equal to the threshold. The range of Min follows the score range. $ $Range can be used to quantify fairness between groups. It is computed as the range of mean group scores . $ $SD can be used to measure between-group fairness , within-group fairness, and individual fairness . SD is the standard deviation of the group mean scores and SD is the standard deviation of users' scores. We compute SD as follows: SD = _{j=1}^{N'} s_j SD where $g_{j}$ is the list of group $j$ users' effectiveness scores and $N'$ is the number of user groups. The range of SD depends on the score range. The weight of group $j$ is $s_j$, the ratio of the group's total scores to the total across all groups. $ $MAD measures between-group fairness as the mean absolute pairwise difference of the groups' mean scores. It is computed follows: MAD = {N' } _{j=1}^{N'-1} _{j'=j+1}^{N'} | - } | where $$ is the group $j$'s mean scores. The range of MAD follows the score range. $ $Gini is a general-purpose inequality measure that has been used to measure between-group fairness , within-group fairness , and individual fairness . Given $N$ non-negative values, $x_1, x_2, , x_N$, the general-purpose Gini is computed as follows: Gini = ^N{ x_j}}{N _{j=1}^N x_j} = ^N{ x_j}}{N^2} Gini is the Gini of the group mean scores and Gini is the Gini of users' scores. We compute Gini as follows: Gini = _{j=1}^{N'} s_j Gini The range of Gini is . $ $CV is a general-purpose statistical measure of data dispersion, which has been used to measure between-group fairness . The general-purpose CV is computed as the ratio of the standard deviation to the mean. We compute CV as follows: CV = , , , })}{mean } The range of CV is $[0, )$. $ $FStat is originally a statistical test that measures the ratio of between-group variance to within-group variance. It has been used to quantify between-group fairness for N' groups and N' users , as follows: V = {N} _{j=1}^{N'} |g_j| ^2 U = {N} _{j=1}^{N'} _{ =1}^{|g_j|} ^2 FStat_= {U/ } where $|g_j|$ is the number of users in group $j$, $_$ is the mean scores of all users, and $x_{j, }$ is the score of the $ $-th user in the $j$-th group. The range of FStat is $[0, )$. $ $KL can be customised to measure various fairness criteria; we select the version where between-group fairness means that effectiveness should be proportional to group size, as it is a popular choice . KL is computed as follows: KL_ = _{j=1}^{N'} p_j _2{{q_j}} p_j = }{ _{j=1}^{N'} } q_j = { _{j=1}^{N'} |g_j|} The range of KL is $[0, )$. $ $ GCE can be used to quantify various fairness criteria ; we choose the version that requires equal utility across groups. We compute GCE as follows:.} GCE_ = -{B } _{j=1}^{N'} p_{ref}^B ^{ } -1 = p_j + c where $B$ is a parameter, $p_{ref}=1/N'$, $p_j$ is as per and $$ is the smoothened value of $p_j$. We set $ =2$, and perform smoothing with $ =0.95$ and $c=10^{-4}$. The range of GCE is $[0, )$. We adopt Atkinson Index , a measure of income inequality from the economics domain , to quantify group/individual fairness for RS users based on recommendation effectiveness disparity. . Given $N$ non-negative values, $x_1, x_2, , x_N$, the general-purpose Atk is defined as follows:.} Atk = 1 - {}f f = ( _{j=1}^{N} { _{j=1}^{N}{w_j}} x_j^{1- } )^{{1- }} where $$ is the mean of the values, $w_j$ is the weight of the $j$-th score, and $ 0$ is the inequality aversion parameter.$, we define $ $Atk $=0$.} Atk with a low $ $ is more sensitive to disparity among higher scores than among lower scores; we compute all Atk with $ =0.5$ . Atk has a -range. To measure individual fairness for $N$ users with Atk , we input the effectiveness score per user to and set $w_j=1, j \{1, , N\}$ to weigh each user equally. As a user's effectiveness score could be 0, we choose $ <1$ to avoid division by zero in $x_j^{1- }$ . Atk is computed as follows: Atk_ = Atk , f , , f ) Essentially, Atk$_$ measures between-group fairness while accounting for the disparity within each group via $f$. As $f$ may be unstable for small groups, we reduce the score contributions from small groups by using the group size as $w_j$ to weigh $f $. To compute $f $ itself, we weigh each user in the group equally. . Atk is subgroup-decomposable , i.e., it can be broken down into between- and within-group components without residual terms , establishing a coherent relationship between individual fairness and group fairness . Specifically, it detects if individual unfairness occurs mainly due to between- or within-group effectiveness disparity.
